A guy who doesn't understand anything about science but can fix everything with duct tape and a screwdriver. He would use everything he could get his hands on. The result might not look pretty but it would work. So whenever we got stuck designing props for chapter 3 we'd ask ourselves: \"Well, how would Bob build it?\""
"Commentary_Title_EYE_HAS_IT"	"OJO CON EL OJO"
"[english]Commentary_Title_EYE_HAS_IT"	"THE EYE HAS IT"
"#commentary\com-eyehasit.wav"	"[Karen Prell] ¿Cómo hacer que un ojo mecánico exprese vida, emociones, que parezca que habla cuando no tiene boca? Los conocimientos del animador sobre el comportamiento humano fueron muy útiles a la hora de animar a Wheatley, la esfera de personalidad. Hablar es mucho más que mover la boca: tienes que usar el lenguaje corporal y el ritmo y los movimientos de la cabeza y los ojos para comunicar motivaciones y sentimientos. Wheatley expresa tranquilidad con movimientos suaves y lentos de la cabeza, la mirada sostenida y la pupila relajada. Si hace movimientos breves y bruscos, parpadea y lanza miradas aquí y allá, transmite nerviosismo o engaño. Si le añades una pupila contraída y un ligero escalofrío, expresa miedo. Si inclina el cuerpo hacia atrás con la vista fija en el jugador es que quiere parecer inteligente, lo que solo engaña al propio Wheatley. La sospecha se expresa entrecerrando los párpados y las asas, que hacen función de cejas y mejillas y son muy expresivas. Además, es divertido recordar al jugador que Wheatley es una máquina. Cuando piratea, los segmentos del ojo y del cuerpo se centran y giran de forma mecánica como los rollos de cinta de un viejo ordenador Univac y, cuando quiere mirar a lo lejos, tiene que girar el ojo hasta el otro lado de la cabeza. Este enfoque de la animación, combinado con el guión y el doblaje, hacen de Wheatley un personaje singular y divertido, medio humano, medio máquina... Mucho ojo y poco cerebro."
"[english]#commentary\com-eyehasit.wav"	"[Karen Prell] How do you make a giant mechanical eyeball express life and emotions, let alone give the impression that he's talking when he has no mouth? The animator's understanding of human behavior came in handy for bringing Wheatley the personality sphere to life. Talking is so much more than just moving a character's mouth. You have to use body language, head attitudes and rhythm of movement and eye focus to indicate a character's feelings and motivations. Slow, smooth head moves, a steady gaze and a relaxed eye aperture indicate that Wheatley is calm. Short, sharp head turns, rapid blinks and glancing around indicate nervousness or deceit. Add a tightly constricted eye aperture and a little shiver to show fear. Tilting the body away while keeping the eye focused on the player signals an attempt at cleverness that ultimately only fools Wheatley himself. Suspicion is communicated by squinting his eyelids and handles, which function as very expressive eyebrows and cheeks. It's also fun to remind the player that Wheatley is a machine. When hacking, his eye and body segments become perfectly centered and spin mechanically, inspired by the spinning tape reels on old Univac computers. And when he wants to look far in front, he flips his eye all the way over to the other side of his head. This animation approach combined with the writing and vocals makes Wheatley quite a unique and entertaining character--part human, part machine, all eye, and no brain."

"[english]#commentary\com-cheating.wav"	"[Phil Co] We wanted to be scientifically accurate in this puzzle, figuring that there'd be a slight delay before you'd see a portal on the moon, since light takes 1.4 seconds to travel the distance. But playtesters would shoot the moon and instantly turn away, thinking nothing had happened. They didn't realize they actually had shot the moon. We tried and rejected a few different approaches to communicate the effect, including a cheat involving quantum entanglement, before settling on the current solution to this problem."

As the blue portal moves around the world, the orange stays rooted. In the original Portal, this room had the portals moving by themselves on a timer. This led to most people simply staring through their orange portal waiting for the blue one to end up in the right place. We felt that altering this to make the players decide where the portals came out was more instructive and meant that players who already knew how to use portals could solve this puzzle both quickly and with authority."

"Commentary_Title_DESTRUCTION"	"DESTRUCCIÓN EN LA INTRODUCCIÓN"
"[english]Commentary_Title_DESTRUCTION"	"INTRODUCTION DESTRUCTION"
"#commentary\com-destruction.wav"	"[Gray Horsfield] La secuencia de la destrucción mientras vas dentro del contenedor planteaba dificultades especiales. La dinámica que el jugador experimenta se computa de hecho en dos simulaciones distintas pero anidadas: la primera es una simulación \"tosca\" de escala, diseñada como un pase de análisis de elementos de tensión. En este pase se computa el movimiento bruto del contenedor y las colisiones y los puntos de ruptura basándose en datos de los keyframes de la trayectoria y en una red de restricciones. A medida que el contenedor se golpea y se rompe, la sala se descompone. Hay más de trescientos cuerpos rígidos y novecientas restricciones integradas, con propiedades de respuesta a la tensión, la fricción y la colisión individualizadas. Esta simulación \"tosca\" recoge la dinámica esencial del recorrido, pero no los detalles. Su resultado se usa para deformar superficies polinómicas que representan la geometría del contenedor y que, a su vez, anteceden a los fragmentos pequeños en forma de cuerpos rígidos anclados. A medida que crece la deformación, los anclajes se rompen y los cuerpos rígidos de los fragmentos entran en la simulación. La simulación detallada incluye también los muebles y los detalles del modelo. Ambas simulaciones se conectan mediante datos de caché y se integran con una serie de scripts. La complejidad computacional de tener dos simulaciones anidadas nos obligó a solucionar ciertos problemas matemáticos. Uno de ellos era que el carácter anidado de las simulaciones creaba inestabilidad en los cálculos de los fragmentos, por limitaciones de cálculo de coma flotante. La solución fue computar los fragmentos en una fase donde la raíz de transformación de la simulación tosca quedaba esencialmente cancelada y almacenada para su uso posterior, lo que nos permitía detectar interacciones más detalladas entre los fragmentos y el entorno. Después de la simulación, la posición y la inercia de la raíz de transformación volvían a aplicarse a los detalles. El problema de computar al jugador en un entorno tan dinámico se resolvió poniéndolo en una sala virtual (oculta en otro punto del mapa) con todas las formas básicas del contenedor renderizado que usamos solo para computar la navegación del jugador. El punto de vista del jugador se enlaza con la transformación de la simulación \"tosca\" y se obtiene el cuadro final renderizado. Al final del recorrido se teleporta al jugador al espacio de juego propiamente dicho. Como las simulaciones son iterativas, pudimos moldear la dinámica en paralelo con el diseño del juego. En el producto final hay más de 1200 cuerpos rígidos, 900 restricciones y 1000 uniones. Sumando todas las iteraciones, el tiempo total de computación de la simulación fue 92,4875 días."
"[english]#commentary\com-destruction.wav"	"[Gray Horsfield] The container ride destruction sequence provided some unique technical challenges. The dynamics you experience are actually computed as two separate but nested simulations. The first is a coarse scale simulation designed as a stress element analysis pass. This pass computes the overall gross motion of the container itself, and computes the collisions and break points based on path keyframe data and a network of constraints. As the container bumps and crashes along, the constraints start breaking, and the room progressively starts to come apart. There are over three hundred rigid bodies and nine hundred constraints in this rig, all individually configured for properties like tensile, friction and collision response. The coarse simulation portrayed gross motion that captured the main dynamics of the ride, but not the fine details. The product of the coarse simulation was then used to deform spline-based surfaces representing the container geometry, which in turn were parents to fine debris as anchored rigid bodies. As the surface deformations increase, anchors are broken and the fine debris rigid bodies are released into the simulation. The fine simulation also includes the interior furniture, and the model detailing. The two simulations were then connected using cache data and were driven together by a series of scripts. Due to the computational complexities of having two nested simulations, we had to come up with some solutions to some interesting mathematical problems. One problem was that the nested nature of the simulations resulted in some instability in the fine debris calculations due to floating point computing limits. The solution employed for this was to compute the fine debris on a stage where the root transform of the coarse simulation was essentially cancelled out and stored for later use. This allowed us to more accurately detect the fine interactions between the debris and the environment. Post simulation, the root transform position and inertia were reapplied to the details. We solved the problem of trying to compute the player within this highly dynamic environment by putting them in a virtual room that has all the base shapes of the rendered container, but is simply used to compute player navigation. (It’s hidden somewhere else in the map.) The viewpoint of the player is then parented to coarse simulation transform, resulting in the final rendered frame. At the end of the ride the player is teleported into the actual game space. The simulations were iterative, enabling us to sculpt the dynamics in parallel with the gameplay design. In the final product there are over 1200 rigid bodies, 900 constraints, and 1000 joints. With all the iterations combined, the actual run time spent computing the simulation was 92.4875 days."

En fin, de todo."
"[english]#commentary\com-reference.wav"	"[Adam Foster] While making Portal 2, we explored our way through vast quantities of reference material. Inspirations included photos of NASA’s Apollo and Shuttle programs, CERN’s particle accelerators both modern and obsolete, industrial robots, derelict Soviet space shuttles, overgrown temples, Brussels metro stations, seedy American motels, junkyards filled with rocketry equipment, Chinese apartment blocks under construction, Polish shipyards, neutrino detectors deep underground in nickel mines, corporate headquarters from a variety of eras, commercial nuclear reactors, experimental fusion reactors, rain-sodden book depositories in Detroit, peculiar cameras, forgotten space probes, you name it."
"Commentary_Title_MONITOR"	"MATA TU TELE"
"[english]Commentary_Title_MONITOR"	"KILL YOUR TELEVISION"
"#commentary\com-monitor.wav"	"[Zachary Franks] En todas las cámaras de pruebas de Wheatley él aparece en un monitor. Al colocarlo en este nivel, uno de nuestros diseñadores pensó que sería divertido que el monitor fuera el objetivo de la placa de fe y se rompiera cuando el jugador o la caja chocasen contra él. Nos pareció buena idea e hicimos que todos los monitores pudieran romperse."
"[english]#commentary\com-monitor.wav"	"[Zachary Franks] In each of Wheatley's test chambers there is always a monitor with Wheatley on it. While placing the monitor in this level, one of our designers thought it would be funny if the monitor was the target of the faith plate and would get broken if the player or the box flew into it. We thought the gag worked well and decided to make every monitor breakable."
"Commentary_Title_NONLINEAR"	"PROGRESIÓN NO LINEAL"
"[english]Commentary_Title_NONLINEAR"	"NONLINEAR PROGRESSION"
"#commentary\com-nonlinear.wav"	"[Greg Cherlin] El plan original de esta cámara era más lineal y no incluía las dos plataformas móviles. Consistía esencialmente en una secuencia de habitaciones por las que el jugador debía avanzar un poco a ciegas, sin saber cuál sería el próximo objetivo hasta llegar. En las primeras versiones había un rompecabezas adicional además del cubo reflector, pero, debido al camino lineal, se tardaba tanto en llegar que los jugadores ya no estaban seguros de qué elementos seguían siendo relevantes, así que optamos por un plan no lineal, condensamos todo el espacio de prueba y colocamos la salida cerca del inicio. De esta forma, ayudamos a los jugadores a darse cuenta de a dónde debían ir y qué objetos les ayudarían."
"[english]#commentary\com-nonlinear.wav"	"[Greg Cherlin] The original layout of this chamber started out much more linear; essentially a sequence of rooms that the player had to progress through somewhat blindly – not knowing what the next goal might be until they got there.  Early versions contained an extra puzzle beyond the reflector cube, but due to the linear path, it took players so long to get to it, that they were confused about which elements were still relevant. This drove us to make the level nonlinear.  To make it easier for players to visualize the puzzle, we condensed the overall test space and moved the exit near the start. This helped the players see where they had to go and which objects would help them get there."
"Commentary_Title_mislead"	"FIJACIONES ENGAÑOSAS"
"[english]Commentary_Title_mislead"	"MISLEADING FIXATIONS"
"#commentary\com-mislead.wav"	"[Jess Cliffe] En las pruebas de este mapa los testeadores se obsesionaban con usar el embudo de traslación para llegar al otro lado y se olvidaban de que tenían la pistola de portales. Para solucionarlo, añadimos el acto destructivo que sucede justo antes de esta cámara y que obliga al jugador a crear un portal desde el suelo hasta una pared para pasar. Una vez dentro, el jugador se encuentra exactamente con la misma situación, pero en un contexto diferente."
"[english]#commentary\com-mislead.wav"	"[Jess Cliffe] While testing this map, we often saw playtesters fixate on the excursion funnel and try to use it to get to the other side, forgetting that they had a portal gun. To remedy this, we added the destruction event that happens just before this chamber. This requires the player to portal through the floor and out of a wall to cross a gap. Once the player enters the test chamber, they are presented with the exact same scenario, but in a different context."
"Commentary_Title_FUNNEL"	"EMBUDO DE TRASLACIÓN"
"[english]Commentary_Title_FUNNEL"	"EXCURSION FUNNEL"
"#commentary\com-funnel.wav"	"[Dave Saunders] En este recorrido por el embudo de traslación, los testeadores a menudo disparaban el portal equivocado en el momento crítico y se mataban, sin entender por qué no se había redirigido el embudo. Ahora, si el jugador ubica un portal equivocado esperando salvarse, le ayudamos situando su otro portal bajo el origen del embudo de traslación. De este modo hacemos que la sección sea a prueba de errores, porque el jugador puede disparar cualquiera de los portales para salvarse."
"[english]#commentary\com-funnel.wav"	"[Dave Saunders] In this excursion funnel ride, playtesters would often end up shooting the wrong portal at the critical moment and killing themselves. This ruined the moment for players, who often didn't quite understand why their excursion funnel hadn't been redirected. As a solution, we now detect when the player places the wrong portal in hopes of saving themselves; we help them out by moving their other portal under the excursion funnel source. This effectively makes the section foolproof, by allowing the player to shoot either portal to save themselves."
"Commentary_Title_WORLD_PORTALS"	"PORTALES DEL MUNDO"
"[english]Commentary_Title_WORLD_PORTALS"	"WORLD PORTALS"
"#commentary\com-worldportals.wav"	"[Eric Tams] Gran parte del éxito de Portal se basa en el placer de esos momentos \"¡Eureka!\" en los que aprendes algo nuevo. Para asegurarnos de que los disfrutas, tenemos que dar un ritmo muy específico al juego. Si fuese demasiado fácil, no lo apreciarías, porque no te parecería que has conseguido nada. Si es demasiado difícil, te sientes estúpido en lugar de inteligente cuando finalmente logras resolver el rompecabezas que tanto te ha costado. El problema es que alcanzar ese equilibrio tan delicado te obliga a barajar mucho los niveles y a modificarlos y retocarlos constantemente. Cuando empezamos el proyecto, introducir un cambio estructural de calado en un nivel o en el orden de niveles exigía horas o incluso días de trabajo para reconectarlo todo y asegurarnos de que todo estaba bien alineado de nuevo. Si queríamos que un proyecto tan complejo como Portal viese la luz algún día con ese equilibrio que buscábamos, necesitábamos poder introducir cambios sin sufrir tantas consecuencias, necesitábamos alterar el espacio, necesitábamos pensar con portales. Los portales hacían posible que cualquier tipo de espacio funcionase. Podías mirar a través de un pasillo a la habitación siguiente, pero esa habitación podría tener una orientación completamente distinta. Podíamos incluir un ascensor, una vista impresionante, una sala más grande por dentro que por fuera, o incluso crear una caída infinita conectando ambos extremos del mismo foso. Muy pronto, todas las conexiones entre distintos espacios eran portales, incluso los alternábamos a voluntad. Una puerta podía funcionar como en los dibujos animados: un simple marco pintado en la pared que se abría sin ninguna dificultad para dar paso a un lugar totalmente distinto. Una vez asentado el juego, pudimos dar los últimos toques a la ruta y eliminamos todos los portales fijos. Solo queda un espacio imposible en todo el juego, ¡a ver si adivinas cuál es!"
"[english]#commentary\com-worldportals.wav"	"[Eric Tams] Much of the fun in Portal is based on the joy of the \"Ah Ha!\" moment when you learn something new. The game needs a very specific pacing to ensure these moments. If things are too easy then you are robbed of that moment since it feels like you didn't accomplish anything. If it's too hard then players feel stupid instead of smart when they finally realize that one small part of the puzzle that they were missing. Unfortunately trying to create that delicate balance leads to a lot of shuffling of levels and a lot of revisions and tweaks to existing levels. When we started the project making any big structural change in a level or the order of levels would lead to hours or even days of busy work trying to reconnect things and make sure they lined up again. If we ever wanted to ship something the size of Portal with the finely tuned balance we desired then we needed a way to be able to make big changes to the layout of the game without paying the cost of making everything line up again. We needed a way to bend space. We needed to think with portals. Using portals to connect different areas in the world we could make any type of impossible space work out. You could look through a hallway into the next room but the hallway might be on the other side of the map and the room you are looking into might be in a completely different orientation. We could seamlessly insert an elevator, a huge expansive vista, a room that was bigger on the inside than the outside, or even create an infinite fall by connecting a shaft back into itself. Soon every connection between any space was a portal. We would even switch them on the fly. Even a simple door worked like the cartoons - just a facade painted on a wall that seamlessly opened somewhere else entirely. Once the game settled down we were able to finalize our path and remove all of the world portals. There's only one impossible space left in the whole game - see if you can figure out where it is."
"Commentary_Title_BLOBULATOR"	"GENERADOR DE GEL"
"[english]Commentary_Title_BLOBULATOR"	"BLOBULATOR"
"#commentary\com-blobulator.wav"	"[Olivier Nallet] La primera implementación del gel se integró en el motor Source allá por 2007. Con los años el código se ha optimizado mucho, pero seguía siendo demasiado lento para las consolas. Los geles eran clave para Portal 2, aunque no sabíamos si funcionarían en consolas. En el verano de 2010 aún dudábamos si optar por una tecnología totalmente distinta para consolas, aunque tuviese peor aspecto. Incluso un gel de muy baja calidad nos ponía al límite de la cuota de rendimiento. Pero realmente queríamos gel de calidad en todas las plataformas. Por otra parte, el código no era apropiado para la SPU de la PS3™. Al final, reescribimos todo el código del gel para sacar partido a múltiples núcleos y SPU y conseguimos lo que queríamos en todas las plataformas sin rebasar las restricciones de memoria y rendimiento."
"[english]#commentary\com-blobulator.wav"	"[Olivier Nallet] The first implementation of the blob was integrated in the Source engine back in 2007. Over the years, the code has been significantly optimized, but was still way too slow to run on game consoles. The blob was a key feature of Portal 2, even though we did not know if we could make it work for consoles. In summer 2010, we were still considering using a completely different tech for consoles--one that would certainly not look as nice. On the 360, even with a very low quality blob, we were barely within our performance budget. But we really wanted to have the same high quality blob among all platforms. Meanwhile, the code was poorly suited for PS3 SPU. We ended up re-writing all the blob code so it would take better advantage of multiple cores and SPUs, giving us quality blobs on all platforms while staying within performance and memory constraints."
"Commentary_Title_FLIGHT"	"TRAYECTORIAS DE VUELO"
"[english]Commentary_Title_FLIGHT"	"FLIGHT PATHS"
"#commentary\com-flight.wav"	"[John Guthrie] Las líneas de trayectoria de la catapulta que aparecen aquí nos permiten visualizar dónde enviará la catapulta al jugador o un objeto físico. Podemos diferenciar la velocidad y la trayectoria de jugadores y otros objetos. Las líneas amarillas para objetos físicos y el verde es la trayectoria del jugador. A veces necesitamos un valor diferente para tener en cuenta la forma de los objetos catapultados: lo que funciona para el jugador puede no funcionar para un objeto, y viceversa. Por ejemplo, a veces una caja aterrizaba en una repisa pero el jugador se daba el gran tortazo. Las herramientas de visualización nos ayudaron a eliminar este tipo de problemas."
"[english]#commentary\com-flight.wav"	"[John Guthrie]  The catapult trajectory lines seen here allow us to visualize where the catapult will deliver a player or a physics object.  We can differentiate the speed and trajectory for players and other objects.  The yellow lines are for physics objects, and green is the player's trajectory.  Sometimes we need to have a different value to accommodate the shape of the objects being catapulted.  What works for the player may not work for an object, and vice versa.  For instance, it was common for a box to make it to a ledge while the player would smack into the side and then fall into the slime. The visualization tools helped us debug these types of problems"
"Commentary_Title_HOTEL_HELL"	"HOTEL INFIERNO"
"[english]Commentary_Title_HOTEL_HELL"	"HOTEL HELL"
"#commentary\com-hotelhell.wav"	"[Randy Lundeen] La idea del personaje criogenizado para siempre en algo que parecía la habitación de un motel cutre llevaba mucho tiempo rondándonos la cabeza, pero no sabíamos cómo sacarlo de allí. Discutimos bastante sobre si la secuencia de la introducción sucedía o no en la imaginación de Chell. Teníamos una secuencia alternativa en la que Aperture había conectado a todos los sujetos de prueba criogenizados a un simulador con una habitación de hotel súper aburrida, de la que Wheatley lo sacaría. Al final la descartamos porque no había tiempo para explicarlo bien y optamos por cambiar la habitación de hotel por un \"paseo dentro de un contenedor\". Esto nos permitía mostrarle al jugador, en lugar de decírselo directamente, dónde habían estado él y los demás sujetos de prueba durante todo este tiempo, indicar el tamaño real de las instalaciones e incluso insinuar cuánto tiempo había transcurrido. Todo esto, a medida que el contenedor se va destruyendo cuando se choca. El resultado es una introducción mucho más dinámica y visceral a Portal 2."
"[english]#commentary\com-hotelhell.wav"	"[Randy Lundeen] The idea of being stuck forever in a state of stasis that looked like a crappy old motel room had been in our minds for a long time, but we weren't sure exactly how we wanted to rip you out of it. There was some debate over whether the opening sequence happened inside the player’s head or not. There was an alternate opening where Aperture had hooked-up all of its cryo-stored test subjects to an incredibly boring hotel room simulator, which Wheatley would then wake the player from. Eventually this was discarded as too difficult to explain in the short time allotted, and we opted to change the hotel room to a “container ride on a rail”. This allowed us to show the player, rather than tell them, about how they and other test subjects have been stored, show some of the scale of the facility and even hint at how much time has passed. We also get to gradually reveal all of this through the destruction of the container itself as it moves and bangs into things. Overall, this gives the player a much more dynamic and visceral introduction to Portal 2."

So it’s hinted at, but not overtly mentioned until the end."

Playtests revealed, though, that it was a bit grueling getting brow-beaten by GLaDOS this early in the game, so her arc was rewritten to give her more of a slow burn towards the player."
"Commentary_Title_MANY_TO_ONE"	"SÓLO PUDO QUEDAR UNA"
"[english]Commentary_Title_MANY_TO_ONE"	"MANY TO ONE"
"#commentary\com-manytoone.wav"	"[Doug Wood] A Wheatley lo concebimos originalmente como un grupo de esferas que el jugador descubriría gradualmente al explorar las instalaciones, pero descartamos la idea por dos razones: primero, que los niveles del interior de las instalaciones debían resaltar las introducciones de cada una de estas nuevas esferas y estos niveles tienen su propia lógica. El número de esferas que queríamos requería demasiados niveles de este tipo, algo que no casaba con Portal; después, tardábamos demasiado en presentar personajes que luego no teníamos tiempo de conocer antes de pasar a la siguiente esfera. Al final decidimos que era mejor conocer bien una esfera que conocer mal seis. Algunas de las esferas las reciclamos y aparecen como núcleos dañados al final del juego."
"[english]#commentary\com-manytoone.wav"	"[Doug Wood] Wheatley was originally envisioned as a group of spheres that you’d discover as you explored the facility behind the scenes. We ditched this idea for two reasons. For one thing, ‘behind the scenes’ levels were required to highlight the introductions of each of these new spheres and these levels have their own unique logic.  The number of spheres we wanted would have made for far too many of these types of levels, resulting in a very un-Portal-like game. Also, we were spending so much time introducing these new characters that we had no time to get to know any of them before they were swept offstage for the next one. Eventually we realized it’d be a lot more satisfying to really get to know one sphere instead of briefly meeting six.  Some of these characters were eventually recycled as corrupt cores for the finale."
"Commentary_Title_ACCENTS"	"ACENTOS HORRIBLES"
"[english]Commentary_Title_ACCENTS"	"HORRIBLE ACCENTS"
"#commentary\com-accents.wav"	"[Erik Robson] La secuencia de la fuga era antes mucho más larga. Wheatley te hablaba con una nefasta imitación del acento americano, porque pensaba que así GlaDOS no podría oírlo, y al mismo tiempo GlaDOS se burlaba de la conversación, ya que por supuesto sí podía. Pero al testear la idea todos los jugadores iban directos a escaparse, así que, o bien abandonábamos el diálogo, o imaginábamos una justificación para retener al jugador cinco minutos aunque podía escapar en cualquier momento... Abandonamos el diálogo."
"[english]#commentary\com-accents.wav"	"[Erik Robson] The breakout sequence here was originally a lot longer, involving Wheatley talking to you in a horrible American accent, assuming GLaDOS can't hear him. Simultaneously, we'd have GLaDOS commenting on the entire ridiculous exchange, because of course she can hear him. When we playtested the concept, every player made a beeline for the opening. So we either had to ditch all the dialogue or figure out a reason for the player to stand around for five minutes even though they could escape at any time. We ditched the dialogue."
"Commentary_Title_CAROLYN"	"CAROLINE"
"[english]Commentary_Title_CAROLYN"	"CAROLINE"

Al principio pensamos en un científico explotado llamado Greg, pero era un derroche contratar a un actor para grabar solo un par de líneas y se nos ocurrió ahorrar usando a la actriz de GlaDOS, Ellen McLain. De repente, nos dimos cuenta de que teníamos la oportunidad de explicar el origen de GlaDOS."
"[english]#commentary\com-carolyn.wav"	"[Dario Casali] The character of 'Caroline' came about because we wanted somebody for Cave to play off of.  Though we had originally envisioned a put-upon Scientist character called Greg, it would have been wasteful to hire an actor for just one or two lines.  Instead, we hit upon the idea of economizing by using GLaDOS-actor Ellen McLain.  Out of nowhere, we suddenly had an opportunity for a GLaDOS origin story."
"Commentary_Title_POTATOS"	"TUBÉRCULOS"
"[english]Commentary_Title_POTATOS"	"POTATOS"
"#commentary\com-potatos.wav"	"[Jay Pinkerton] GlaDOS-patata fue el personaje más difícil de concebir. Fue el último que se grabó. Siempre habíamos querido darle la vuelta a la tortilla para la secuela, hacer que GlaDOS se asociase contigo a regañadientes contra un enemigo común, pero la realidad no funcionaba tan bien como la idea. Que un villano omnipotente te dé guerra durante las pruebas, pase. Que lo haga una patatita que llevas pegada en la pistola es otra cosa. Los testeadores acababan hasta las narices y deseaban arrancarla y, la verdad, no podíamos reprochárselo, así que nos enfrentamos a la perturbadora idea de convertir a la malvada GlaDOS en algo más humano y cercano, lo que, desde el punto de vista del guión, fue una de las tareas más difíciles del juego."
"[english]#commentary\com-potatos.wav"	"[Jay Pinkerton] The character of PotatOS was actually the hardest one to crack for us, and was the last character to get recorded. We’d always wanted to switch things up for the sequel, so that GLaDOS would become your unwilling buddy cop partner against a new threat, but this ended up being far more entertaining as a concept than in execution. It’s one thing to have an omnipotent villain chastising you while you’re testing. It’s quite another to have a little potato on your gun doing the same. Playtesters wanted to tear her off the gun, and we didn’t blame them. So we were faced with the unsettling prospect of making dry, robotic supervillain GLaDOS more human and relatable. This ended up being one of the hardest writing jobs in the game."
"Commentary_Title_BIRDBRAIN"	"CABEZA DE CHORLITO"
"[english]Commentary_Title_BIRDBRAIN"	"BIRDBRAIN"
"#commentary\com-birdbrain.wav"	"[Erik Wolpaw] Una de las principales dificultades argumentales de GlaDOS-patata era que, si ahora es tu colega y tenéis un objetivo común, ¿por qué no te ayuda a superar las pruebas? La respuesta real es, obviamente, que si lo hiciera el juego no tendría gracia. Una de las soluciones que se nos ocurrió fue que el pájaro del tercer acto fuese dándole picotazos y llevándose porciones de su inteligencia, por lo que no te podría ayudar; pero resultó imposible ponerla en práctica por razones técnicas. Aun así, algunos seguimos recordando la idea del pájaro con ternura."
"[english]#commentary\com-birdbrain.wav"	"[Erik Wolpaw] One of the larger plot holes that PotatOS introduced was: She’s your buddy now. She’s got the same goals as you. So why isn’t she helping you solve the tests? The real answer is, obviously, that if she did help you solve the tests there’d be no game. One solution we we came up with was for the bird from Act 3 to keep swooping in and pecking bits of her off your gun. As the bird kept eating the part of her that knew how to solve the tests, PotatOS would actually get dumber over time. For technical reasons, this ended up being impossible to execute, so we ended up finding another solution. Some of us though will always have a place in our heart for the bird solution."

Fortunately, Stephen Merchant did a great job of intercutting funny bumbling Wheatley with occasional outbursts of power-mad, villainous Wheatley."

On the other hand, simply letting him out of the GLaDOS body with a slap on the wrist seemed unacceptably anticlimactic. Having him sucked out into space with the Space Sphere seemed like a happy middle ground that everybody could get behind."
"Commentary_Title_WHIMSY"	"IDIOSINCRASIA ROBÓTICA"
"[english]Commentary_Title_WHIMSY"	"ROBOT WHIMSY"
"#commentary\com-whimsy.wav"	"[Mike Belzer] Para los diseños originales de los robots y las primeras pruebas partimos de la premisa de que, siendo robots, sus movimientos debían ser mecánicos, sin mucha \"personalidad\". Sin embargo, la reacción de los testeadores en las pruebas y de los fans ante los vídeos de avance nos convenció de que un enfoque más idiosincrásico daría mejores resultados, y abandonamos ese enfoque frío para experimentar con distintas personalidades y gestos, lo que nos abrió todo un mundo de posibilidades."
"[english]#commentary\com-whimsy.wav"	"[Mike Belzer] In our original robot designs, and the first animation tests, we started with the premise that, being robots, their movements should have limited 'personality' and be a bit more mechanical. The feedback of our game testers, and the reaction of fans through the trailers, convinced us that a more whimsical approach to animation would lead to a better experience. So we moved away from a sterile approach and found a whole new range of possibilities, as we began to explore their personalities and gestures."
"Commentary_Title_ORANGE"	"ROBOT NARANJA"
"[english]Commentary_Title_ORANGE"	"ORANGE BOT"
"#commentary\com-orange.wav"	"[Keith Lango] Hemos oído decir que el robot naranja parece delicadito, y es verdad que tiene algo de pájaro, incluso algo del personaje Félix Unger de La extraña pareja. El bailecito tipo \"me estoy haciendo pis\" cuando está inactivo se basa en los robots bípedos actuales. Como ellos, el robot naranja, con su diseño más bien inestable, tiene que estar constantemente en movimiento para mantener el equilibrio."
"[english]#commentary\com-orange.wav"	"[Keith Lango] The orange bot has been described as finicky, and you can see elements that are somewhat birdlike and even reminiscent of the Odd Couple's Felix Unger. The 'gotta pee' idle dance was born out of the idea that current bipedal robots are constantly needing to readjust in order to find their balance. Because Orange's design is rather unstable, the motion design solution was kind of a perpetual motion needed to maintain his balance."
"Commentary_Title_BLUE"	"ROBOT AZUL"
"[english]Commentary_Title_BLUE"	"BLUE BOT"

We accomplished this with signs, props and materials. In this area, we get a glimpse of what Aperture Science must have been like in its heyday. We used rich, warm colors and materials like carpeting and marble to indicate that Aperture had a lot of money at one time. Cave Johnson and his staff were just starting out in their enterprise and everyone was eager to do science. This stands in contrast to the stark, cavernous environment outside and the materials you see in the Aperture Labs from later decades. As the player makes their way through more recent areas of the lab, the materials become cheaper, with an abundance of plastic and linoleum showing that Aperture had fallen on harder times and could no longer build high-quality offices for its workers."

"Commentary_Title_PREDICTION"	"PORTALOMANCIA"
"[english]Commentary_Title_PREDICTION"	"PREDICTING WITH PORTALS"
"#commentary\com-prediction.wav"	"[Dave Kircher] Como los niveles en Portal 2 son mucho más complejos que en Portal, hemos tenido que reforzar los algoritmos de movimiento del jugador. El jugador se representa mediante una caja alineada axialmente (AABB) en el mundo, lo cual es un problema para la teleportación, que casi nunca tiene alineación axial. Para solventarlo, trazamos el jugador como la AABB que se usaría en cada lado simultáneamente y combinamos los resultados de forma que podamos utilizarlos. Hay que predecir muchas más cosas que en juegos anteriores con Source, porque los portales y las entidades proyectadas modifican el modo en que el jugador recorre los escenarios. El proceso de predicción en sí supone un dolor de cabeza cuando usas portales. Si ya teníamos que enfrentarnos a un espacio no lineal, con los portales tenemos además que lidiar con un tiempo no lineal en un espacio no lineal."
"[english]#commentary\com-prediction.wav"	"[Dave Kircher] The levels in Portal 2 are much more complex than in the first game, and as a result we’ve had to beef up the player movement algorithms. The player is represented as an axis-aligned box in the world, which creates a problem for portal teleportation because portal teleportation is almost never axis-aligned. To improve how we handle this, we trace the player as the axis-aligned bounding box they would use on each side of a portal simultaneously and merge the results into something usable. We have to predict quite a bit more than previous Source Engine games because portals and projected entities change the way the player moves through the world. Prediction itself is a mind bending headache when dealing with portals. We’re already dealing with a non-linear space. Now we also have to deal with non-linear time in a non-linear space."
"Commentary_Title_HUB"	"DISEÑO DE LA CENTRAL"
"[english]Commentary_Title_HUB"	"HUB DESIGN"
"#commentary\com-hub.wav"	"[Matt T. Wood] Definir la estructura de la Central planteaba retos nuevos y específicos del modo cooperativo. Partimos del supuesto de que los jugadores han llegado a diferentes fases del juego con distintos compañeros. Empezar en un punto neutro como la Central garantiza que cuenten con un momento y un lugar para coordinarse, decidir dónde van a ir y visualizar sus datos respectivos. Los jugadores necesitan información clara de su progresión en el juego. Buscando soluciones a estos requisitos, nos pareció importante no sacar al jugador del espacio de juego con menús e interfaces exteriores. Por eso, los jugadores pueden ver claramente su estatus sobre las puertas y navegan entre los niveles recorriendo el propio espacio tridimensional. GlaDOS no sólo está ahí para vincular el modo en solitario con el cooperativo, sino también para aclarar los objetivos y la información de este último."
"[english]#commentary\com-hub.wav"	"[Matt T. Wood] In laying out the structure of our Hub, we had a number of challenges that were new and specific to co-op play. We have to assume that each player has made different progress throughout the game, playing with different partners, and starting in the neutral center of the hub was a way of making sure that they had a chance and place to coordinate. Players needed a way to agree on where to go, and way to visualize each other's completion data. As they played, they needed concrete feedback on how much progress they had made. In seeking solutions, we felt it was important to keep the player in the gamespace rather than resorting to menus and UI that would take them out of the game. Therefore, players can clearly see their status over the doors, and they navigate by walking the course in the 3D space. GLaDOS is there to connect the experience to the singleplayer game, while holding the player's hands a bit to keep the co-op goals and feedback clear."
"Commentary_Title_HUB_RETURN"	"REGRESO A LA CENTRAL"
"[english]Commentary_Title_HUB_RETURN"	"RETURN TO THE HUB"
"#commentary\com-hubreturn.wav"	"[Gautam Babbar] Nuestro objetivo inicial al crear la Central era que el jugador pudiese probar distintos tipos de juego. Si se atascaba o quería descansar, podría explorar otra cámara u otra mecánica. En realidad, esto impedía darles suficiente entrenamiento y limitaba el alcance de los rompecabezas que podíamos diseñar. Con una estructura tan abierta, es más difícil introducir mecánicas nuevas porque no se puede garantizar que el jugador sepa qué debe hacer para resolver un rompecabezas. Con una estructura lineal, sin embargo, podemos saber con seguridad qué es lo que ya conoce el jugador y ofrecerle una experiencia mejor y con más ritmo, y una historia que se hace cada vez más trepidante."
"[english]#commentary\com-hubreturn.wav"	"[Gautam Babbar] Our initial goal in designing the hub was to provide players with a lot of choices, so they could to sample a variety of game types.  The idea was that if they got stuck or needed a break, they could pick another test chamber or another mechanic to explore.  In reality, this prevented us from doing sufficient training and limited the overall scope of the puzzles.  With such a flat structure, it was much harder to layer old mechanics onto new ones, because we couldn't guarantee that players understood what they needed to know to solve a puzzle.  By going linear, we could guarantee prior knowledge and provide a much better experience, more satisfying pacing and a story that gathered momentum over a long period of time."
"Commentary_Title_STEALING"	"ROBAR EL NÚCLEO"

Pensamos en cambiarla, pero los testeadores nos dijeron que era su preferida. Además, era la más utilizada, porque solo necesitaba un jugador para iniciarse y era la más fácil de usar. Al final, acabamos adoptando su funcionamiento para todas las demás bromas de equipo."
"[english]#commentary\com-stealing.wav"	"[Ted Backman] We have a taunt where one robot steals the other's core. When implemented, it was the only taunt that required one person to initiate. Because of this we were afraid that it would be used for griefing. We were in the process of rethinking our approach when early playtesters rated this as their favorite taunt.  It was also the most used team taunt because it only required one player to initiate, and was easier to use than the others.  We ended up changing all the other team taunts to emulate the way stealing the core works."
"Commentary_Title_PING_TOOL"	"HERRAMIENTA MARCADOR"
"[english]Commentary_Title_PING_TOOL"	"PING TOOL"
"#commentary\com-pingtool.wav"	"[Josh Weier] Enseguida nos dimos cuenta de que intentar señalar a tu compañero dónde ir, dónde mirar o dónde poner un portal iba a ser muy difícil. Incluso con chat de voz, decir \"ahí\" no basta para dar indicaciones en un espacio en 3D. Los jugadores querían levantarse y apuntar directamente en la pantalla del compañero, por eso desarrollamos la herramienta Marcador. Es tan importante que decidimos que lo primero que íbamos a hacer era enseñar a los jugadores a utilizarla, de manera que aplazamos la aplicación de la mayoría de las acciones del jugador para que centrara toda su atención en el aprendizaje de la herramienta. Por eso los robots cooperativos empiezan separados en un tubo, sin poder siquiera andar ni disparar portales."
"[english]#commentary\com-pingtool.wav"	"[Josh Weier] Early on we realized that trying to tell your partner where to go, where to look or where to place a portal was going to be really hard.  Even with voice chat, saying 'over there' doesn't give enough information to your partner within the 3D space.  Players kept wanting to get up and point directly at their partner's screen.  We developed the ping tool to address this problem.  Because the ping tool was so important, we decided to train players in its use before we did anything else.  We held back on the vast majority of player actions and let the players focus entirely on the ping tool.  This is why the co-op bots start separated in a tube, without even the ability to walk or shoot a portal."

Due to lag and other issues, syncing up actions over voice chat turned out to be rather difficult.  Therefore we created the countdown timer as a way for players to keep in sync.  It's completely predictable.  Both players see the GO at the same instant, and the clocks run in sync even if they are on different systems."

Before trying it for the first time, they would discuss their plan as if it were some unique or special new game mechanic.  When it just worked, they were overjoyed."
"Commentary_Title_DYING"	"MORIR TODO EL RATO"
"[english]Commentary_Title_DYING"	"DYING ALL THE TIME"
"#commentary\com-dying.wav"	"[Andrea Wicklund] Morir en un juego suele considerarse un fracaso. En Portal, sin embargo, la muerte es parte de la experiencia de resolver los rompecabezas. En el modo cooperativo, no solo mueres más a menudo, también puedes morir a manos de tu compañero (de forma puramente accidental, por supuesto). Pensamos que la muerte debía ser algo normal e incluso divertida. Probamos unas animaciones muy trabajadas, por ejemplo una de un machacador que aplastaba lentamente tu robot, pero eran repetitivas y restrictivas, y los jugadores acababan prefiriendo no arriesgarse a tener que esperar a que acabara la animación. Tuvimos que buscar una muerte que fuese rápida pero visceral, gratificante y divertida y que estimulase la creatividad en el juego."
"[english]#commentary\com-dying.wav"	"[Andrea Wicklund] Normally in games, when players die, they see this as a big failure.  But in Portal, death is a normal part of the puzzle solving experience.  In co-op, death not only happens more frequently, but it can happen at the hands of your partner (purely accidentally of course).  We felt it was important to not only make death \"no big deal,\" but to make it fun.  Early on, we tried some elaborate death animations--such as showing your robot slowly getting crushed under a giant crusher.  These were awesome to watch, but they quickly became repetitive.  Also, after a very short while, players grew afraid to take risks.  The fear of having to wait a long time before trying again prevented them from simply playing and experimenting in a spirit of fun.  We had to find the right balance where death was quick enough to be a non-penalty, and elaborate enough to be visceral and satisfying--a fun pay-off for creative play."
"Commentary_Title_DISASSEMBLY"	"DISPOSITIVO DE DESMONTAJE"
